**MM DR. T.GANAPATI SASTRI  
(1860-1926)**



**GENERAL INTRODUCTION**

***KAUṬALYA — HIS IDENTITY***

***The names***

 There is no other name in the history of ancient Indianpolitical thought which commands as much popularity andrespect as that of the author of *Arthaśāstra*. With exception tothe heroes of the epics and Purāṇas no name was morefamiliar to Indians than that of Kauṭalya. If the work isconsidered as an enigma as some writers hold, the very nameof the author itself can be considered so. What exactly is thefamily name of the author which is often prefixed, almostinseparably, with that of the treatise? One does not know.

 The credit of bringing this great work to light for thefirst time goes to Dr. R Shama Sastri, who was then theLibrarian (subsequently Curator) of the Oriental ResearchInstitute, Mysore. Towards the beginning of 1909 he broughtout the first edition. In his introduction he quotes *Viṣṇupurāṇa*IV. 24 and states that the author of the treatise was a Brahminscholar named Kauṭalya.

***Kauṭalya or Kauṭilya?***

 The name of the author of *Arthaśāstra* is found mentionedin numerous works in Sanskrit literature. Both the formsKauṭilya and Kauṭalya are found used as synonyms. ButShama Sastri, the first editor of the treatise has preferred theterm Kauṭilya with an ‘i’. The passage quoted from *Viṣṇupurāṇa*also supports him. He quotes :

तान्नन्दान् कौटिल्यो ब्राह्मणस्समुद्धरिष्यति । तेषामभावे मौर्याश्च पृथिवीं भोक्ष्यन्ति । कौटिल्य एव चन्द्रगुप्तं राज्येभिषेक्ष्यति ।

His quotations from *Nandisūtra*, *Abhidhānacintāmaṇi* ofHemachandra and *Vaijayantī*of Y*ā*davaprakāśa too contain theform Kauṭilya.

 Since both the forms Kauṭilya and Kauṭalya are met within literature Western scholars do not attach much significanceto this aspect and they hold that it is only a minor Variation.

***Kauṭalya - the correct name***

* *But it was T. Ganapati Sastri who for the first time established that Kauṭalya must be the correct form. He basedhis conclusion on the correct readings of the text preserved inthe Southern manuscripts of *Arthaśāstra*. In fact when thedifference in the name came to his notice Ganapati Sastricorrected his edition after the first 40 pages had been printed.He observes¹: “Kauṭilya (इकार मध्य) is certainly a misnomer.For, neither the term Kauṭilya, nor its root Kuṭila is explainedin Nighaṇṭus as Gotrarṣi and crooked. On the other hand, theword Kuṭala is mentioned by Keśavasvāmin in his *Nānārthārṇavasaṁkṣepa* as meaning both Gotrarṣi and an ornament”. Fromthis it is clear that Kauṭalya is the correct name having for itsroot Kuṭala”. Further, he maintains that all the manuscripts—five of them—read throughout as Kauṭalya.

 K. Sambasiva Sastri who succeeded Ganapati Sastri tothe office of the Curator of the Trivandrum Manuscripts Library in his introduction to the Malayalam commentary of*Arthaśāstra* has offered some interesting remarks on this aspectof the problem.² He has quoted the *Jayamaṅgalā*commentary on*Kāmandakīyanītisāra* as follows: कौटल्य इति गोत्रनिबन्धना विष्णुगुप्तस्य संज्ञा।

He observes that the form कौटल्य can be derived as कुटलस्यगोत्रापत्यम् Further *Śabdārthacintāmaṇi* states: कौटल्यःवात्स्यायनमुनिः.Referring to the passage quoted by Ganapati Sastri from*Nānārthārṇavasaṁkṣepa*, Sambasiva Sastri observes that Kuṭalais only an ancient sage from whom a line of succession ensued.But that does not prove that Kauṭalya, the author of *Arthaśāstra*was a scion of that line. Further he holds that the assertion ofGanapati Sastri that Kauṭalya is the correct name is only a

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

¹TSS 79, Trivandrum, 1924, introduction, p. 4

²Kauṭalīya with Malayalam commentary, Part I (second edition), University, of Kerala, 1972, introduction, p. 22

*General Introduction*

novel exercise and it be treated as such. Sambasiva Sastrifurther promises a detailed discussion on this aspect in thesecond volume of his publication to be issued shortly. Thereinhe promises further evidence in this regard. But in a shortprefactory statement to the second volume he admits that freshand incontrovertible evidence is yet to be collected and that itis reserved for the third volume.³As fate would have it, by thetime the third volume was issued he retired from the office ofthe Curator. His successor V.A. Ramasvami Sastri quotes a
portion from the Malayalam commentary which he issued as the third part of the project and tries to justify the stand of Ganapati Sastri.⁴The verse found in the commentary:

**ऋणमादौमनुर्ब्रूते वास्तुकं भार्गवो मुनिः।**  
**बृहस्पतिस्तु निक्षेपं विवाहं कुटलान्वयः॥**

‘Manu commences Vyavahāra with *ṛṇadāna*, Bhārgava with *vāstuka*, Bṛhaspati with *nikṣepa* and the descendant of Kuṭala with *vivāha*’; if it can be accepted as authentic, throws much light on the vexed question whether the name of the author of *Arthaśāstra* is Kauṭalya or Kauṭilya; for by the Sūtra : गर्गादिभ्यो यञ्(Pāṇini VI. 1. 105) we get the form Kauṭalya inthe sense of *Kuṭalānvava* and not Kauṭilya”. He further observesthat all editions of the work except that of Ganapati Sastri adoptthe reading Kauṭilya. He considers it possible that the usage ofViśākhadatta, the author of *Mudrārākṣasa* that : कौटिल्यः कुटिलमतिःetc; might have influenced the editors to accept the formKauṭilya.

 Still modern writers use both the forms. This is evidentfrom the vast literature on *Arthaśāstra* that has been producedduring the last few decades. Rādhākānta in his Ś*abdakalpadruma* is inclined to treat both the forms as having reference togotra name. He states:

अस्तु कौटल्य इति वा कौटिल्य इति वा चाणक्यस्य गोत्रनामधेयम् ।

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

³Kauṭalīya with Malayalam commentary Part II (second edition), Trivandrum, 1972, Perface

⁴Kauṭalīya with Malayalam commentary Pārt III (second edition), Trivandrum, 1973, Preface, p. 1

From all this it is evident that both forms were prevalent inearly days. Hence it is very difficult to prove one or the otherform is wrong. It is to be noted that the name Kauṭalya occursseveral times in the *Arthaśāstra*.

***Cāṇakya***

 The author of *Arthaśāstra*is popularly known as Cāṇakyaon account of his having been a native of the Caṇaka region.Legendary accounts maintain that since he was born at Caṇakain the Punjab he was called *Cāṇakya*.⁵The view propoundedby Ganapati Sastri in this regard has found acceptance at thehands of most of the modern writers. It is observed: “In theauthoritative judgment of Ganapati Sastri, the author of*Arthaśāstra* was named as Kauṭalya, because he was of Kuṭalagotra and since he was born at Caṇaka, he was called Cāṇakya,and his parents baptised him as Viṣṇugupta”. Some commentators of Nāmaliṅgānuśāsana of Amarasiṁha have referred to*Arthaśāstra* mentioning its author as Cāṇakya. Kṣīrasvāminrefers to the work as : चाणक्योक्तोऽष्टदण्डः. The *Ṭikāsarvasva* ofSarvānand contains the statement: चाणक्ये तु रथ्याप्रतोल्योर्भेदः.Thus the identity of Cāṇakya and Kauṭalya is established beyond any doubt.

 It is interesting to note that Kauṭalya has not mentioneda region called Caṇaka despite the fact that he has referred toabout 75 places all over India in addition to the names of17 hills and 14 rivers. But it is a fact that he had referred toPañcanada as follows:

**सौराष्ट्रिकाःपाञ्चनदा-  
     स्तेषां प्रत्यवराः स्मृताः।**

He states that the war elephants of Saurāṣṭra and Punjab areof the lowest quality and that one must be aware of this.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

⁵TSS 79 introduction, p. 4; Usha Mehta and Usha Thakkar Kauṭilya and his Arthaśāstra, Delhi, 1980; p. 2

⁶M.V. Krishna Rao, Studies in Kauṭilya, (third edition), Delhi, 1979, p. 2

⁷Arthaśāstra, Mysore. 1960 -55

Many consider him to have spent his early days in Punjabfrom where he migrated to the court of Chandragupta It isobserved⁸: “The loyalty of the foreigners in Punjab and theadjoining regions was doubtful and a dangerous example ofinsecurity and perfidy was set by Alexander, one of the world’sgreatest conquerors. All this left a deep impression on theminds of both Kauṭalya and Candragupta. Kauṭalya’s conceptof *vijigīṣu* and his description of a king on march seem to beinfluenced by this factor”. But it is often conjectured that helived in Magadha, a place mentioned by him in enumeratingweights and measures. “The account that Kauṭilya gives ofagriculture has close resemblance to the conditions in SouthBihar. His reference to the size of the villages and their population, the forest tribes of central India, preference for traderoutes to the South of India— all go to confirm the view that helived in Magadha”, observe some scholars.

 Hemachandra the lexicographer who compiled the*Abhidhānacintāmaṇi* suggests that the name Cāṇakya is givento the author of *Arthaśāstra* since he happened to be the son ofCaṇaka. Hemachandra states : कौटिल्यश्चणकात्मजः. Thus thelexicographer, for the first time, supplies the name of Kauṭalya’sfather.

 Anyway it is fairly logical to assume that the nameCāṇakya has some connotation with the native place ofKauṭalya. It may be noted that the name does not occur anywhere in the Arthaśāstra. But the ancient MalayalamCommentator of *Arthaśāstra* who probably belonged to the12th century A.D has identified Kauṭalya with Cāṇakya. Inexplaining the passage : षाड्गुण्यमेवैतद् अवस्थाभेदाद् इति कौटल्यःoccurring in the VII Adhikaraṇa he observes¹⁰:

“Guṇaṅṅalārumula; avasthābhedattināl bhinnaṅṅalāyolle;

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

⁸Usha Mehta & Usha Thakkar, op. cit. p. 9

⁹Ibid, p. 3

¹⁰BhāṣāKauṭalīyam, ed. K.N. Ezhuthachan, University of Madras, Madras, 1960, p. 294

enṭu cāṇakyan pakṣam; avasthābhedamāvitu svarūpabhedam”.

***Viṣṇugupta***

 The name given to the author of *Arthaśāstra* by his fatherduring the *nāmakaraṇa* ceremony was believed to have beenViṣṇugupta meaning ‘protected by Viṣṇu’. Daṇḍin in his*Daśakumāracarita* has mentioned this name as follows:

 अधीष्व तावद् दण्डनीतिम् । इयमिदानीमाचार्यंविष्णुगुप्तेन मौर्याय षड्भिःश्लोकसहस्रैस्संक्षिप्ता ।

 In the beginning of the *Nītisāra* of Kamandaka this nameis referred to in glorious terms. After recounting the exploits ofthe scholar it is stated :

**नीतिशास्त्रामृत धीमानर्थशास्त्रमहोदधेः।  
य उद्दध्रेनमस्तस्मै विष्णुगुप्ताय वेधसे॥**

 The name is mentioned only once in the *Arthaśāstra* andthat occurs in the colophon to the work.

**दृष्ट्वाविप्रतिपत्तिं बहुधा शास्त्रेषु भाष्यकाराणाम् ।  
स्वयमेव विष्णुगुप्तः चकार सूत्रं च भाष्यं च ॥**

***Other names***

 Kauṭalya, Cāṇakya and Viṣṇugupta are the three namespopular in literature. But some lexicographers have enumeratedseveral more names without disclosing their source. As a resulta few more names have been preserved. The passages of thelexicographers may be noted here.

 Hemachandra, the author of *Abhidhānacintāmaṇí* states :

**वात्स्यायनो मल्लनागःकौटिल्यश्चरणकात्मजः ।  
द्रामिलःपक्षिलस्वामी विष्णुगुप्तोङ्गुलश्च सः॥**

 Yādavaprakāśa in his famous *Vaijayantīkośa* observes :

**वात्स्यायनस्तु कौटिल्यो विष्णुगुप्तो वराणकः।  
द्रामिलःपक्षिलस्वामी मल्लनागोऽङ्गुलोऽपि च॥**

 Nāmamālikā of Bhoja offers yet another string of namesas follows :

**कात्यायनस्तु कौटिल्यो विष्णुगुप्तो वराणकः।  
द्रामिलःपक्षिलस्वामी मल्लनागोऽङ्गुलोऽपि च॥**

It seems that all the above three lexicographers depended onalmost the same source for enumerating the names.Theadditional names provided by them are :

\(1\) Vātsyāyana,  
(2) Mallanāga,  
(3) Caṇakātmaja,  
(4) Drāmila,  
(5) Pakṣilasvāmin,  
(6) Aṅgula,  
(7) Varāṇaka, and  
(3) Kātyāyana

Since Vātsyāyana is given as another name of Kauṭalya, A.B.Keith has propounded the view that the author of *Arthaśāstra*and *Kāmaśāstra* are one and the same. Since there are manycommon terms in both the texts, the Western scholar felt thatthey are by the same hand. But Indian scholars like ShamaSastri and Ganapati Sastri have rejected the theory afterexamining it in detail.

 The name Drāmila has given rise to the speculation thathe was a native of the Drāviḍa country, the south India. Sincealmost all the manuscripts of the *Arthaśāstra* and its commentaries were procured from Kerala, it is often held that Kauṭalyamust have been a native of the land. Further at least fiveancient commentators of the text belong to the place. Kūṭallūrfamily of Nampūtiri Brahmins in Kerala had a long traditionand the name of the family is similar to that of the gotra ofKauṭalya. It is conjectured that some ancestor of the familywent on a pilgrimage and settled in Magadha assisting Chandragupta in his political designs.

 As regards the other names there is nothing to substantiate by way of cross references in Indian literature. Hence wemay conclude with Ganapati Sastri in holding that the authorwas known popularly under the aliases Kauṭalya, Cāṇakya, andViśṇugupta.

***DID KAUṬALYA EXIST?***

 The very existence of Kauṭalya has become a moot pointamong some Western scholars. Winternitz. Jolly and Keith are

the protagonists of this theory. They hold his name as a symbol of political strategy and treachery. The fact that Kauṭalya’s name is not referred to by Patañjali in his *Mahābhāṣya* is taken as further strengthening their view. Jolly evenwent to the extent of asserting that *Arthaśāstra* is a piece ofliterary forgery and that it must be the work of a Pandit givento pedantic classifications and definition rather than that of apractical statesman of the stature of Chandragupta’s minister.While Shama Sastri has offered suitable explanations to someof their points, T. Ganapati Sāstri took upon himself thetask of giving a convincing rebuff. The following is the gistof the different views :

 **(1)** Kauṭalya has never mentioned the empire of Chandragupta in the course of the treatise. So the capital city ofPāṭaliputra does not find a place among the numerous regionsreferred to by him.

 **(2)** The political strategy enumerated by Kauṭalya befits small countries or principalities rather than that of anempire.

 **(3)** Megasthenes, the Greek historian has given a good account of the empire of Chandragupta, but he failed to mention the name of Kauṭalya, the real architect who helped hisking to become an emperor.

 **(4)** Since the name of Kauṭalya is often mentioned inthe course of the text as representing a particular school ofthought, he cannot be the author of the treatise itself. Furtherthe views of Kauṭalya are stated to have been dismissed byother authorities in the text.

 **(5)** The name Kauṭalya suggests an unsavoury meaningof crookedness and hence the author would not accept it.

 All these points have been discussed by Ganapati Sastrithread-bare, establishing at once the authorship as well as theearly date of the treatise. The salient features of the repliesgiven by him were :

 **(1)** Though Kauṭalya has not mentioned that he had administered the empire of Candragupta he has stated at the

end of the text that it was he who usurped the country fromNandas on behalf of the Maurya emperor. Hence the nonmention of Pāṭaliputra is not a major ground to deny him thecredit.

 Further, the present writer may point out that Kauṭalyahas revealed his knowledge of Pāṭaliputra in referring to ariver flowing nearby. In dealing with the origin of pearls hedescribes a variety called *pāśikya—*obtaining from the river Pāṣikā. An ancient Malayalam commentator points out thatthe river flows in the proximity of Pāṭaliputra as follows:

 “Pāśikyamāvo pāṭaliputranarike ullitoru āṭṭil ulavāmavu”. This is translated by Ganapati Sastri in his commentary as:पाशिक्यं पाटलिपुत्रान्तिकगतायां पाशिकाख्यायां नद्यामुत्पन्नम् ।

 **(2)** It is true that most of the principles enumerated applyto small states. But the strategy enumerated in the 15th and16th sections of the 7th Adhikaraṇa has special reference to anempire.

 **(3)** Megasthenes might have met Chandragupta long after
Kauṭalya’s service came to an end. The Greek historian ismore interested in eulogising his patron rather than taking hiscredit away by mentioning the role of Kauṭalyain the formation of the empire. Further Megasthenes“*Indika”*is preservedonly in fragments and the portion dealing with Kauṭalya might be missing at present.

 **(4)** In Sastraic treatises it is usual to cite opposite viewsfirst and then to establish the desired view after disproving theaccuracy of the first. Similarly, there is no harm in enumeratingone's own view first and then pointing out the views of theopposition, as is done by Kauṭalya on several occasions.

 **(5)** Words like Kautsa are used as names as in the*Raghuvaṁśa* of Kālidāsa, though the radical meaning may beunsavoury. The word Kauṭilya with an “i”may be such a word, but what we have uniformly in the text is Kauṭalyameaning the descendant of Kuṭalagotra. It has no such stigmaas to warrent its omission.

 It is in these lines that the Trivandrum editor offers hisremarks against the views of European scholars. Apparentlyhis views are valid and supported by convincing arguments.

 Thus there is ample evidence to establish the authenticityof Kauṭalya and his treatise, There are numerous referencesto the author and his work in Jain, Buddhist and Brahmanicalliterature.

***LIFE AND TIMES***

 Regarding the life of Kauṭalya only traditional accountsare available. Hemachandra, the lexicographer states thatCaṇaka was his father. The Brahmin scholar must havesuffered indignity at the hands of the rulers of the Nandadynasty and it was more than sufficient to prompt him tousurp the kingdom in favour of his disciple Chandragupta. Thestory of the usurpation and restoration of thecountry hasformed the subject matter of several works in Sanskrit.The *Kathāsaritsāgara* of Somadeva, *Mudrārākṣasa* of Viśākhadatta and *Cāṇakyakathā* contain the story in some details.Works like Somadeva's *Nītivākyāmṛta* and Daṇḍin’s*Daśakumāracarita* mention his resolution by taking revenge onthe Nandas.

*Native place*

 Regarding his native place there are several theories.According to Ganapati Sastri he was a native of Caṇaka inPunjab and the name Cāṇakya implies this. The name Drāmilaa variant of Drāviḍa is taken to suggest his home land inthe South. According to one view he originally belonged toKāñcipuram and that he was a Drāviḍa Brahmin sporting histuft in front. His journey to the north was in search of livelihood. Kerala too has staked its claim for the birth place of this celebrated author. His reference to the river Cūrṇīat theoutskirts of the modern town Koṭuṅṅallūr is also mentionedin this connection. According to a tradition, Viśṇugupta anative of Kerala went to Vārāṇasī on pilgrimage where he losthis daughter somehow. Refusing to return to Kerala he foundrefuge in Magadha and later rose to the position of being the

preceptor of Chandragupta. The Kūṭallur Nampūtiri family of Kerala is supposed to be his original home.

 Cāṇakya's place of birth remains a matter of controversy.The *Mahāvaṁśaṭīkā*, a Ceylonese Buddhist work mentions Takṣaśilā (Taxila) as his birth place. A place called Gollaviṣaya has also been mentioned as the birth place of the authorof *Arthaśāstra*. A considered view in this regard is suggested bythe same authority as follows: “Since Alexander’s campaignswere predominantly in the Punjab and Plutarch has gone onrecord that Alexander had met Chandragupta as a youth duringhis campaigns, it would be safer to accept the version thatTakṣaśilā (Taxila) in the Punjab was the native city of Cāṇakyawhere he and Chandragupta spent several years together.¹¹

***Times***  
 Kauṭalya seems to have lived in a turbulent period. Thecountries around were in political doldrums and suffering frommal-administration inviting foreign invaders like Alexander tocapitalise on the anarchical situation. The Nandas were on theverge of self-destruction and the neighbouring rulers were in nobetter position to give stability to the land. A strong hand wasneeded to stabilize the country. Opportunity presented itselfbefore a capable political thinker and a practical administratorlike Kauṭalya and he seized it with a view to bettering the lifeof the common man. He succeeded in his aim and the results ofhis strategy are found codified in his treatise. His intention wasto change the age-old political practices and to usher in a newadministrative system suitable to the prevailing conditions andsuggesting ways and means to meet all eventualities.

***Personality***

 Jawaharlal Nehru in his *Discovery of India* has given acorrect assessment of this great personality that changed thepolitical map of ancient India. “Cāṇakya has been called theIndian Machiavelli and to some extent the comparison is

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

¹¹V.K. Subramonian, *Maxims of Chanakya,* New Delhi 1980, p. 2

justified. But he was a much bigger person in every way,greater in intellect and action. He was no more a follower ofking, a humble adviser of an allpowerful emperor. Bold andscheming, proud and resourceful, never forgetting a slight,never forgetting his purpose, availing himself of every device todelude and defeat the enemy, he sat with the reins of empire inhis hands and looked upon the emperor more as a loved pupil than as a master. Simple and austere in his life, uninterested in the pomp and pageantry of high position, when he redeemedhis pledge and accomplished his purpose, he wanted to retire,brahminlike, to a life of contemplation. There was hardly anything Chāṇakya would have refrained from doing to achievehis purpose; he was unscrupulous enough, yet he was alsowise enough to know that this very purpose might be defeatedby means unsuited to the end”.

 Any careful reader of the *Arthaśāstra* will arrive at theabove conclusion.

***DATE***

 Once the existence and identity of Kauṭalya are establishedit is easy to fix the date. Shama Sastri observes that “fromIndian epigraphical researches it is known beyond doubt thatChandragupta was made king in 321 B.C and that Aśokavardhana ascended the throne in 296 B.C. It follows, therefore,that Kauṭalya lived and wrote his famous work, the *Arthaśāstra*somewhere between 321 and 300 B.C.”¹²T. Ganapati Sastri hasendorsed the view generally after discussing the variousconnected issues.

 Western scholars like Jolly, Keith and Winternitz suggesta later date to the treatise based on the points of differencebetween the accounts of Megasthenes and Kauṭalya. Accordingto them the present treatise is a compilation from Kauṭalya'soriginal work by some one prepared at a much later period.From a consideration of similar evidences these scholars maintain that the present *Arthaśāstra* could not have been written

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

¹²Kauṭilya's Arthaśāstra, English trans. by Dr. R. Shama Sastri, (8th edn.), 1967, preface, p. vii.

earlier than the third century AD. Parhaps the only Indian scholar to come closer to their view was R.G. Bhandarkar who suggested 2nd century A.D. as the probable date of composition.

 Ganapati Sastri in dealing with the issue has touched the main points of their contention and has answered them to the best of his ability. In the three concluding stanzas to his Sanskrit introduction to the third volume of his edition of *Arthaśāstra* he has summarised hisfindings.¹³He concludes that the treatise was written by the minister of the Maurya king and there is no evidence worth the name to disprove this.

***WORKS***  
 It is not definitely known as to how many works were composed by Kauṭalya. But there is evidence to postulate that he must have written a few more than his famous book on politics.

** (1)** *ARTHAŚĀSTRA* on the science of polity is easily his *magnum* opus both in size and conception. It is at once authoritative and magnificent in all details.

** (2)** *DHĀTUKAUṬALIYA* a work on minerals is attributed to the authorship of Kauṭalya by Bhaṭṭasvāmi who wrote the commentary of Arthaśāstra styled *Pratipadapañcikā.* In dealing with the duties of the superintendent of mines Kauṭalya qualifies the authority as : शुल्बधातुशास्त्ररसपाकमणिरागज्ञः। Shama Sastri in his English translation splits the first part as*Śulbadhātuśāstra* and misses the significance of the reference. Ganapati Sastri following Bhaṭṭasvāmi’s commentary has correctly distinguished the two sciences and states : धातुशास्त्रं धातुभ्यःसर्जिकागन्धकादियुक्त्या सत्त्वपातनोपदेशकं धातुकौटल्यादिशास्त्रम् ।  
Further, the last stanza of the *Arthaśāśtra* states:

**येन शास्त्रं च शस्त्रं च नन्दराजगता च भूः।  
अमर्षेणोद्धृतान्याशु तेन शास्त्रमिदं कृतम् ॥**

Ganapati Sastri explains the word “śāstra” as having reference

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

¹³TSS 82, p. 7

to this particular work and observes **:** येनेत्यादि । शास्त्रं धातुकौटलीयादिकमितरच्छास्त्रजातम्

 The Malayalam commentator also defines *Dhātuśāśtra* separataly. From all this it seems that Kautalya had written a work dealing with the science of minerals.

** (3)** *A WORK ON ASTROLOGY* is found attributed to Viṣṇugupta by Varāhamihira in his Bṛhatsamhitā with the prefatory statement : उक्तं आचार्यविष्णुगुप्तेन ।  
He also quotes a stanza from this astrological work. The verse in question has been noticed by both Shama Sastri and Ganapati Sastri.

** (4)** *KAUṬALIYAM***:** yet another work styled *Kauṭalīyam* implying the authorship of Kauṭalya is seen referred to by the author of the *Jayamaṅgalā* commentary on the *Kamasūtra* of vātsyāyana. With a prefactory note : तद् यथा कौटलीयम् a stanza is quoted by the commentator. This again is pointed out by T. Ganapati Sastri in his introduction referred to above.

** (5)** *CĀṆAKYA-RĀJANĪTI* is a collection of maxims and aphorisms ascribed to Cāṇakya. A definitive edition of the text has been published by Ludwik Sternbach from Madras in 1963. Collections of these maxims are generally known as *rāja-nīti-śāstra* or *rāja-nīti-samuccaya*, or epitomes of polity. These exist in different forms and under different titles. In the various collections one to five introductory stanzas are made to say that Cāṇakya is the real author. Scholars are of the opinion that such collections are of a late origin, even though the aphorisms may have their origin at the hands of Cāṇakya.

 Thus it is known that Kauṭalya has written works on a variety of subjects. In the course of his political treatise he has quoted nearly ten predecessors with whose works he was familiar.

***ARTHAŚĀSTRA-THE MAGNUM OPUS***  
 Treatises on the science of governmental administration have been produced well before the times of Kauṭalya, and he himself has admitted that he has perused most of them. Further he has quoted the views of nearly ten of his predecessors in the

course of his work. His main contribution was to bring order out of the maze of writings which had preceded him. He often summarises, criticises and improves upon the writings of earlier authorities and in this respect he is an original thinker. One may even say that he was the greatest political thinker that India had ever produced.

 Kauṭalya begins his treatise with the statemant that it is conceived as a compendium of almost all the *Arthaśāstras* that have been composed by ancient teachers. Though modest in his claim he has in fact superseded his predecessors by the accuracy and planning of the work. It has all the salient features of a well-planned and executed thesis. Chapters are thoughtfully planned and subdivided well in advance. The work consists of 15 Adhikaraṇas, 180 Prakaraṇas, 150 Adhyāyas and 380 Kārikās. Altogether there are 6000 ślokas each consisting of 32 syllabic units. This is inclusive of the prose passages of the text. The above-mentioned plan has been disclosed by the author at the beginning of the text itself so as to give a comprehensive idea of the contents. This can be represented as follows :

|        |                  |                    |                  |                 |
|:------:|:----------------:|:------------------:|:----------------:|:---------------:|
| **15** | **Adhikaraṇas**  | **180 Prakaraṇas** | **150 Adhyāyas** | **380 Kārikās** |
|   1    |  विनयाधिकारिकम्  |        1-18        |       1-21       |      1-48       |
|   2    |  अध्यक्षप्रचारः  |       19-56        |      22-57       |     49-120      |
|   3    |   धर्मस्थीयम्    |       57-75        |      58-77       |     121-151     |
|   4    |   कण्टकशोधनम्    |       76-88        |      78-90       |     152-174     |
|   5    |    योगवृत्तम्    |       89-95        |      91-96       |     175-190     |
|   6    |    मण्डलयोनिः    |       96-97        |      97-98       |     191-196     |
|   7    |   षाड्गुण्यम्   |       98-126       |      99-116      |     197-285     |
|   8    | व्यसनाधिकारिकम् |      127-134       |     117-121      |     286-304     |
|   9    |  अभियास्यत्कर्म  |      135-146       |     122-128      |     305-312     |
|   10   |  साड्ग्रामिकम्   |      147-159       |     129-134      |     313-324     |
|   11   |   सङ्घवृत्तम्    |      160-161       |       135        |       325       |
|   12   |     आवलीयसम्     |      162-170       |     136-140      |     326-330     |
|   13   |  दुर्गलम्भोपायः  |      171-179       |     141-145      |     331-340     |
|   14   |   औपनिषदिकम्   |      177-179       |     146-149      |     341-377     |
|   15   |  तन्त्रयुक्तिः  |        180         |       150        |     378-380     |



In addition to this there is a concluding stanza stating that Viṣṇugupta himself has composed the aphorisms as well as the gloss since the early texts were found to be confusing.

 Some editors have divided the text into three parts. The first part is named *Tantrādhikāra* in which the first five Adhikaraṇas are included. In the second part called *Āvāpādhikāra* are included eight Adhikaraṇas from six to thirteen. The last two Adhikaranas are included in the third part which they designate as the remaining two *Bhāgadvayaśeṣarūpaḥ*. Such a three-fold classification seems to be implied by the text. The *Śrīmūlam* commentary of Ganapati Sastri has pointed out this as follows :¹⁴

 तन्त्रं नाम स्वराज्यक्षेमसाधनानुकूलः पञ्चस्वाद्येष्वधिकरणेषु पञ्चनवत्या सूत्रैः प्रतिपाद्यमानोऽर्थः। आवापःशत्रुराष्ट्रहरणानुकूलः षष्ठादिषु त्रयोदशान्तेषु अष्टस्वधिकरणेषु सूत्राणामेकाशीत्या प्रतिपाद्यमानोऽर्थः।  
There seems to be no specific term to denote the last two Adhikaraṇas. The 14th Adhikaraṇa is commented by Ganapati Sastri with the following introductory remark :¹⁵  
इत्थं त्रयोदशभिरधिकरणैस्तन्त्रावापौ प्रपञ्चितौ, श्रथ तदुभयानुकूलमौपनिषदिकं चतुर्दशमधिकरणमारभ्यते ।

 It is not possible to give the contents of this compendium in a nut-shell. So vast is the range of subjects treated by the author. The work is an illustration par excellence of a scientific approach to the problems of politics. All the requirements and criteria of an exact science are kept in view by the astute author. Regarding the mode of treatment it is observed:¹⁶ “There is the statement of prima facie view, *Pūrvapakṣa*, and the rejoinder, *Uttarapakṣa,* and the conclusion *Ekānta :*and in the determination of a conclusion, all the different steps involved in reaching it are adopted. Facts are discussed with reference to place, procedure, doubt, implication, contrariety, *Viparyaya,* *Vākyaśeṣa,* Anumata, *Vyākhyāna*, *Nirvacana*, *Nidarśana* and *Anāgatāvekṣaṇa*; there are references

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

¹⁴TSS 79, p. 5

¹⁵TSS 82, p. 213

¹⁶M.V. Krishna Rao, op. cit, p. 2

to previous portions as well as to the alternatives, *Vikalpa* and *Samuccaya* and *Ūhya*. Likewise, words in their different connotations, *Arthāpatti*, implications and application *Atideśa* are examined; also, the author has used his own technical terms, *Svasaṁjñā* with a view to explain recondite and complex political phenomena.”

 Kauṭalya has not only depended upon the texts on polity available to him but also on the rich experience he has gathered while being the kingpin of the usurpation of the country from the Nandas. His vital role in this has become a legendary account of great halo. His work contains references to a period which is often regarded as that of Alexander’s invasion of India. He seems to have been impressed by the glorious past of India and the subsequent misery and destruction caused by the campaigns of the Greek invader. The invasion had weakened the small states and the free tribes of Punjab and Sindh who meekly became the victims of oppression without offering even a semblance of real fight. Naturally Kauṭalya wanted his king to guard against such invasions and usurpations. He gives a warning note to the ruler illustrating the tragedies befallen on the lives of Dāṇḍakya, Bhoja, Karāla, Videha, Ajabindu, Sauvīra, Haihaya and Tālajaṅgha who were the rulers who existed before the time of Kauṭalya. Keeping these instances in mind Kauṭalya wrote his treatise. He was the preceptor of Candragupta as Aristotle was of Alexander. It is possible that the Indian counter-parts had some contact with the Greek invader. “Legends reveal that Kauṭalya was a student of Taxila and that he as the companion of Candragupta contacted Alexander in one of his campaigns in the valley of the Punjab and came back to his original home determined to emulate the example of the great conqueror. The progress of a conquering king *Vijigīṣu* like that of Alexander was indelibly impressed on the mind of both the preceptor and the pupil and a detailed description of an ideal king on the march is manifest in many Adhikaraṇas of *Arthaāstra*.”¹⁷Kauṭalya has stated in his treatise that it was

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

*¹⁷Ibid*, p. 13  


written for the benefit of his own king :

**कौटल्येन नरेन्द्रार्थे शासनस्य विधिः कृतः।**

 Comparison between Kanṭalya and Aristotle is inescapable. Both exhibited the same degree or arlessness of intellect, the same passion for truth and the courage to put faith in

reason. “Like Aristotles *Poetics*, the *Arthaśāstra* reveals classic clearness of outline and precision of form. There is reflection as well as observation[^1].”¹⁸Both laid stress on the importance of individuality and individual responsibility. Both believed that keeping aloof from office and political activity is not expected from a worthy man. It is often held that Kauṭalya’s took resembles the notes of an official who was in the thick of administrative responsibilities, for it reveals an allround experience based on practical knowledge. His remarks on agriculture betray the conditions that existed in Pāṭaliputra in Magadha where he must have lived the better part of his life. Most of the principles enumerated in his compendium have become the guiding lines of administration during his own time. It has become an authoritative text book for princes down the centuries.

[^1]: "M.V. Krishna Rao. d. 22"

***COMMENTARIES ON ARTHAŚĀSTRA***  
 There are several commentaries on this important text on polity. Seven of them are considered to be standard commentaries. Of these six are in Sanskrit and one in Malayalam. In addition to these there are several recent commentaries in Hindi. Still we are at a loss to find the exact connotations of many of the passages of the text. The following are the available commentaries :

**(1)** ***Pratipadacandrikā***

 This commentary by Bhaṭṭasvāmi is noticed by Shama Sastri and is made use of in fixing the text of the first edition issued in 1909. The commentary as available to him extended from the 8th Adhyāya of the II Adhikaraṇa upto the end of that section. In other words the commentary was available for the last 29 chapters of Adhyakṣapracāra. It was written

on palm leaves using the Grantha script suggesting a Kerala origin. The same manuscript deposited with the Madras Oriental Manuscripts Library has been made use of by T. Ganapati Sastri also for his *Śrīmūlam* commentary. The available portion of the commentary has been published by K.P. Jayaswal and A. Banerji Sastri through the Journal of the Bihar (and Orissa) Research Society, Patna, during 1925- 26.¹⁹

 It seems, that the original manuscript on palm leaf belonged to the Kūṭallūr Nampūtiri family in Kerala from which a copy was transcribed for the Madras Manuscripts Library. In the same library, a manuscript written in Telugu script also is found deposited and it is compared and collated with the Kerala manuscript.²⁰

 Nothing worthwhile is known about the author Bhaṭṭasvāmi. The commentary is rather brief and to the point. It is Bhaṭṭasvāmi who assigns *Dhātukauṭalīya* a work on minerals to the authorship of Kauṭalya. Ganapati Sastri has extensively made use of this gloss for his researches on *Arthaśāstra*. It is interesting to note that an anonymous writer of the 12th century has composed a Malayalam commentary on the text and it shows striking resemblance to the passages of Bhaṭṭasvāmi. Ganapati Sastri has expressed the opinion that the Malayalam commentator has closely followed Bhaṭṭasvāmi. Since both the commentators are from Kerala it is not possible to prove as to who borrowed ideas from whom, maintains K. Sambasiva Sastri in his introduction to the edition of the Malayalam commentary. If Bhaṭṭasvāmi happens to be the borrower, then he is to be placed later than the 12th century A.D.

**(2)** ***Nayacandrikā***

 The second commentary called *Nayacandrikā* is attributed

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

¹⁹Ed. K.P. Jayaswal & A. Banerji Sastri, JBORS, IX Pt. I, March, 1925, pp. 1-24; June 1925, pp. 25-54; Sept. & Dec. 1925, pp. 55-92; XII Pt I March 1926, pp. 93-130; June 1926, pp. 131-168; Sept. 1926; pp. 169-214.

²⁰D. 3873 of Govt. Oriental Manuscripts Library, Madras.

to the authorship of Vādikavicūḍāmaṇi Mahopādhyāya Śrī Mādhavayajvamiśra. Once again the original manuscript was procured from Kerala from the library of Ampalakkāṭṭu Karunakara Menon. The Madras Manusripts Library prepared a copy from the manuscript between 1917-18 and that copy with the number R 2403 has formed the basis for its publication by Jolly and Udayavira Sastri in 1924 from Lahore.²¹ It seems that the Madras copy was compared with an original palm leaf manuscript belonging to Mānavikrama Anujan Kunchunni Raja of Samūtirikovilakam in Kerala.

 The manuscript is incomplete and it extends only from the 7th Adhyāya of the VII Adhikaraṇa upto the 4th Adhyāya of the XII Adhikarana. Thus the commentary is available for 37 Adhyāyas from 103 to 139 of a total of 150 Adhyāyas of the *Arthaśāstra.* In other words, it covers practically 6 Adhikaraṇas of a total of 15. We have no information about the author, though his name is mentioned in the colophon as Mādhavayajvamiśra. But he seems to have composed a commentary on the Prākṛt mahākāvya *Setubandha*, a copy of which is available in the Madras Manuscripts Library. In a colophon to this commentary the name of Mādhavayajvan is mentioned along with the title Vādikavicūḍāmaṇi-mahopādhyāya. This shows that he was a versatile commentator.

 Both Shama Sastri and Ganapati Sastri have made use of this commentary, the former for fixing the text and the latter for preparing the *Śrimūlam* commentary. Shama Shastri has calculated that the commentary comes to 4000 ślokas or as many syllabic units of 32 letters. Since the copies of the commentary have been solely procured from Kerala it is believed that Mādhavayajvan must be a native of the region. The date of the commentary could not be fixed satisfactorily, for want of adequate evidence.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

²¹The Arthaśāstra of Kautilya, A new edition by J. Jolly and R. Schmidt, Panjab Sanskrit Series, Lahore, 1923 and 1924; Vol. I Text; Vol. II Notes with the commentary Nayacandrikā,

**(3) *Jayamangalā***  
  This is a new commentary not noticed by Shama Sastri or Ganapati Sastri. It extends only upto the end of the first Adhikaraṇa. In other words, the commentary is available only for the first 21 Adhyāyas of the Arthaśāstra. It has been edited and printed from Madras²². The edition is based on the transcript No. R 5208 of the Madras Manuscripts Library. The transcript was prepared in 1925-26 from a palm leaf manuscript of Paliyam Library of Cennamangalam in Kerala. The editor conjectures that the commentator might be identical with the one who commented upon the Nītisāra of Kāmandaka. In that case he must be Śańkarārya.

 There is some misunderstanding about the authorship of the Jayamangalā commentary. Since the commentary happened to be transcribed in the same book in which the Caṇakyatīkā of Bhikṣuprabhamati is also copied, some scholars entertained the belief that Jayamangalā too is by the same hand.²³ But then there is no reason why the author should call the first part as Jayamangalā and the second part as Cāṇakyatīkā.

**(a) *Cāṇakyatīkā***

 Yet another incomplete commentary on Arthaśāstra is available at present. From a colophon at the end of the manuscript it is gathered that the author is one Bhikṣuprabhamati. This is found copied in transcript No. 5203 of the Madras Manuscripts Library immediately after the Jayamangalā commentary. This too has been obtained from Kerala. It has  
been printed by G. Harihara Sastri from Madras. The  
\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

²²Ed. G. Harihara Sastri with introduction, Kuppusvami Sastri Research Institute, Madras, 1958. pp. 76

²³Ullur S. Parameswara Iyer. *Keralasāhityacaritram,* Trivandrum, 1957, Vol. I, pp. 268-69; K. Sambasiva Sastri, *Kauṭaliya* with Malayalam commentary; I, pp. 9, 28

²⁴*Cānkyaṭikā* of Bhikṣuprabhamati, ed. G. Harihara Sastri, Journal of Oriental *Research*, Madras, Vol. 26, 1956-57

commentary concerns only with the second Adhikaraṇa and thefirst Adhyāya of the third Adhikaraṇa. In other words, the commentary is available for 37 Adhyāyas of the *Arthaśāstra*from 22 to 58.

 Since all the above-mentioned four commentaries were procured from Kerala, it is believed that their authors might have flourished in that country.

**(5) *Nītinirṇīti***

 A Jain scholar Ācārya Yogghama alias Mugdhavilāsa has commented on the work. His commentary is available only partially. It extends from the first Adhikaraṇa to the beginning of the second. In a colophon it is found:  
इति मुग्धविलासाङ्कयोग्धमविरचितनीतिनिर्णीत्यभिधानायां कौटलीयराजसिद्धान्तटीकायां अध्यक्षप्रचारे प्रथमोऽध्यायः।  
The available portion has already been published fromBombay.²⁵ The commentary is noteworthy in that it is the first of its kind to be unearthed from the north India. The text is more detailed than hitherto known and D.D. Kosambi who contributed an introduction to the volume concludes that the original *Arthaśāstra* must be at least 25 percent bigger than the already available text.²⁶ According to him scribes must have omitted many portions since the text was out of vogue for quite some time.

 These are the early Sanskrit commentaries that have been unearthed by scholars. The possibility of coming across other commentaries is still there.

**(6) *Bhāṣā-kauṭalīyam***

 By far the most significant commentary on the text is a a Malayalam commentary of an early date. No particular name

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

²⁵A Fragment of Kauṭalya’s *Arthaśāstra* alias *Rājasiddhānta* with a fragment of the commentary named *nītinirṇīti* of Ācārya Yogghama alias Mugdhavilāsa; ed. Muni Jina Vijaya, Singhi Sanskrit Series 47, Bombay. 1959.

²⁶D.D; Kosambi,“The text of Arthaśāstra” II. of the American Oriental Society, New Haven, Vol. 78 (1958).    

is given to it by its anonymous author. It is available from the biginning upto the end of the 7th Adhikaraṇa. In other words a total of 116 out of 150 Adhyāyas of the *Arthaśāstra* is commented upon in the available portion. The commentary as available has already been printed in parts.²⁷ The first three were issued from Trivandrum and the last from Madras. The first volume brought out by K. Sambasiva Sastri consists only of the first Adhikaraṇa called Vinayādhikārikafor which he has used three manuscripts.(i)A palm leaf manuscript from the Trivandrum Palace Library collections consisting of 1 to 7 Adhikaraṇas.(ii) Transcript from Madras Manuscripts Library consisting of 1,3,5,6 and 7 Adhikaraṇas, (iii) A manuscript collected from Kūṭallūr Brahmadattan Nampūtirippad extending from second Adhikaraṇa and 5,6, and 7. The second volume is also edited by K. Sambasiva Sastri for which the above mentioned manuscripts were based. The third volume of the commentary edited by V.A. Ramasvami Sastri again is based upon the manuscripts used for the first two volumes. In addition to this he has used two manuscripts giving the text alone. The fourth volume is a critical edition based on sound editorial principles. The manuscripts used were almost the same. By the time original manuscripts were copied and the editor has made use of such copies too. As a result it has become very difficult to trace the original manuscripts from which copies were made.  
\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

27\.

 i) The *Kauṭilīya* with a Malayalam commentary, Pt.I (Vinayādhikārikā), Ed. K. Sambasiva Sastri. Travancoren Manuscripts Library, Trivandrum, 1930, reprinted in 1972

 ii) The *Kauṭilīya* with a Malayalam commentary, Pt.II (Adhyakṣpracāra). ed. K. Sambasiva Sastri. Trivendrum, 1938, reprinted in 1972

 iii) The *Kauṭilīya* with a Malayalam commentary, Pt. III (Dharmasthīya), ed. V.A. Ramasvami Sastri. Trivandrum, 1945; reprinted in 1973

iv) *Bhaśākauṭalīyam* (from Kaṇṭakaśodhanam upto the end of Sāḍguṇya ),ed KN. Ezhuthachan, University of Madras,1960

 By far the most complete manuscript is the one collected by the Palace Library, Trivandrum which is now deposited with the Kerala University Manuscripts Library. It contains all the seven Adhikaraṇas hitherto noticed.

 The credit of noticing this invaluable commentary for the first time goes to Shama Sastri. In his preface to the second edition of his English translation of the text brought out on 10 July 1923 he mentions this important commentary. His Sanskrit introductions are silent about this work. He has actually procured a copy of the commentary extending from third to seventh Adhikaraṇas though he could not make use of it to revise the English translation. The first two Adhikaraṇas of the commentary were not obtained by him at the proper time. It seems that he did not appreciate the value of this very useful commentary to the extent it deserves. The following are his observations²⁸ :

 “All along, it has been my earnest endeavour to revise my translation with the aid of two more commentaries on the work since discovered. One of them is *Nayacandrikā* by Mādhavayajvan and the other a translation, or rather paraphrase, by an unknown author, in a mixture of Tamil and Malayalam languages. Unfortunately both of them are as incomplete as Bhaṭṭasvami’s. commentary. The first extends from the seventh chapter of the Seventh Book to the end of the third chapter of the Twelfth Book. It is neither a word-by-word commentary like Bhaṭṭasvāmi’s nor a paraphrase like the Tamil-Malayalam commentary. Mādhavayajvan, its author, satisfies himself by supplying some connecting links between successive chapters and successive paragraphs in each chapter. The Tamil-Malayaam paraphrase is, on the other hand, very copious, but omits to notice obscure passages here and there. It extends from the beginning to the end of the Seventh Book. Written as it is in a mixture of dialects, it is not very easy to understand it. It

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

²⁸*Kauṭilya’s* *Arthaśātra* , Eng. trans. by Dr. R. Shama Sastri,(8th edn), Mysore 1967, perface to the second edition , p .xxiii

seems to be quite recent and is not free from misinterpretationof a few words. The word “Pravahaṇa” for example, is taken to mean a drinking bout instead of a shipping company. There is however, no doubt that, as they cover almost the whole text, when put together they will be of great help in clearing obscurities with which the text abounds......As I could not get in time a copy of the Tamil-Malayalam commentary on the first two Books, the most important portion of the work, and as demand for the translation was on the increase, I had to hurry on the second edition without comparing my own translation with that of the Tamil commentator”.

 It is evident that Shama Shastri had some misconceptions about the commentary. The first is that it is a Tamil-Malayalam commentary using a mixture of Tamil and Malayalam languages or written in a mixture of dialects. In fact as Ganapati Sastri observes : “it is an ancient Malayalam version”. K.N. Ezhuthachan observes that it is not the work of a Tamil scholar domiciled in Kerala, on the other hand, it exhibits the genius of ancient Malayalam language known only to a Keralite. Only a scholar conversant with the genius of Malayalam could attempt such a magnificent literary effort²⁹. In fact the language of the commentary compares well with that of ancient Malayalam inscriptions. Ullur S. Parameswara Iyer, the foremost historian of Malayalam literature, qualifies the work as a varitable mine of diamonds for the students of Malayalam language³⁰.The view of Shama Shastri that it is a recent work is also not correct since the work is teeming with usages that bear the stamp of ancient Malayalam as testified by early inscriptions. K. Sambasiva Sastri has even conjectured the possibility of this Malayalam commentary being the source of the Sanskrit commentaries of Bhaṭṭasvāmi and Mādhavayajvan.”³¹He is of

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

²⁹. *K.*N. Ezhuthachan, *bhāṣākauṭalīyam,* introduction p.xxxii.

³⁰.Ullur S.Parameswara Iyer, *Keralasāhityacritam*, Vol.I P.270

³¹.The *Kauṭaliya* with Malayalam commentary pt.I, introduction p.11

the opinion that Malayalam commentary need not necessarily be a translation of the Sanskrit original. It can be vice-versa as in this instance since the Sanskrit commentaries mentioned above belong to Kerala.

 Shama Sastri has found fault with the Malayalam commentator for explaining “Pravahaṇa” to mean “a drinking bout”³². The original passage of the text is: प्रवहणनिमित्तमेकोऽमात्यः सर्वानमात्यानाबाहयेत्।

Shama Sastri translates the passage as “with the intention of sailing on a commercial vessel (prahavaṇanimittam) a minister may induce all other ministers to follow him”³³. In a foot note he adds “sea-faring merchants cross the high seas by means of Pravahaṇas”. N.S. Venkatanath- acarya who brought out the fourth edition of the text from Mysore in his notes observes”³⁴। जलस्थलयोः प्रयाणे साधनभूतं नौदोलादिकं प्रवहणमित्युच्यते । अत्र तु तत्सहायेन क्रियमागजलक्रीडादिकम् अभिप्रेतम् ।

 It is almost clear that the successor of Shama Sastri has based his notes on the commentary of T. Ganapati Sastri who observes as follows³⁵ प्रवहणनिमित्तं प्रवहणं नौविशेषःकर्णीरथश्च,तेन जलस्थलयात्राक्रीडोद्यानभोजनादिकं गोष्ठीकौतुकं लक्ष्यते तदर्थमित्यर्थः।

 Here is an interesting situation. Ganapati Sastri has seen the English translation of the passage of Shama Sastri and the natural meaning of the word ‘Pravahaṇa’is a ‘boat’or ‘seafaring vessel’. Then why did he add the meaning ‘bhoja- nādikam’ in his comments? This is the real position. The Malayalam commentary utilised by Ganapati Sastri
states:³⁶“Prītibhojananimittamāyoru amātyan ellā amātyakaleyum viḷiccu kūṭṭuvitu". It means that the minister may invite other ministers for a gathering of feast. In fact Ganapati Sastri has incorporated the meaning given by Shama Sastri as well as the one by the Malayalam commentator by extending the scope of

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

³².English translation, p. xxiii

³³. *Ihid*,p16

³⁴. Mysore edition 1960, p.17

³⁵. *TSS*79, p. 45

³⁶. *K*. Sambasiva Sāstri, *op. cit*, pt. I, p. 94



his comments. Actually the Malayalam commentator had in his mind the word “Prahavaṇa” and not “Pravahaṇa” as printed in the text by the Trivandrum editor. The meaning of Pravahaṇa is surely ‘a boat’, but Prahavaṇa means a ceremonial feast. By metathesis, the scribe has wrongly noticed the wordas Pravahaṇa which led Shama Sastri, Ganapati Sastri and others to translate as ‘boat’ or ‘sea-faring vessel’. K.N. Ezhuthachan has correctly pointed out that the text must read as “Prahavaṇa” in the above instance.³⁷To establish his point beyond doubt he quotes the commentary styled *Jayamaṅgalā* where the passage is explained as³⁸: प्रहूयन्तेऽस्मिन् स्वजनमित्रारणीति प्रहरणम् उद्वाहादिरुत्सवः।

This shows that the text of the passage as well as the comments of the two editors are defective. Hence the Malayalam commentator deserves our gratitude in giving the correct reading (which Shama Sastri has unwittingly given in brackets in his English translation quoted above). Further it is interesting tonote that the word “prahavaṇa” occurs again in the *Arthaśāstra* under the section on Kaṇṭakaśodhana. The passage is: प्रहवणेषु योगसुरामन्तान् वा ग्राहयेयुः।

In this instance all the commentators including the Malayalam commentator have given the correct connotation. While Shama Sastri gives the translation as “while under intoxication due to the drinking of medicinal beverage on the occassion of religious festivals”, the Malayalam commentator states”³⁹: “anṭiye prahavaṇamenpitu tuṣṭibhojanam”. Following him Ganapati Sastri comments the passage as : प्रहवणेषु तुष्टिभोजनादिषु।From this it is clear that the ancient Malayalam commentator is accurate as well as authentic in his comments.

 It may be noted that Kauṭalya has used the word Pravahaṇika when he statcs वस्तुच्छिद्रानुलासेषु श्रेणीप्रवहणिकनिकाया आवसेयुः।

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

³⁷. *Bhāśākauṭalīyam*, introduction, p. xxxi

³⁸. *Ibid.*

³⁹. *Ibid* p.54

All commentators give meaning ‘merchants coming from foreign lands’ — a meaning already given by the Malayalam commentator. It is almost difinite that Ganapati Sastri had based his meaning on the passage of the ancient Malayalam commentator.

 It is a pity that Shama Sastri could not make adequate use of this excellent ancient commentary in Malayalam at the time of the third edition of his English translation published in the year 1929. Obviously he was misinformed about the reliability of the gloss. Still here and there he has added some footnotes noting the view of the commentator. It is to be noted that the Malayalam commentary does not comment on many words or phrases in the Sūtras. It is difficult to say whether the uncommented portions do not form the parts of the sūtra text or whether they are not commented upon simply because they are self-explanatory. At times one is forced to think that the available text is incomplete in certain portions. for instance the section⁴⁰ : क्षुद्रपशूनां काष्ठादिभिर्दुःखोत्पादने पणोद्विपणो वा दण्डः। शोणितोत्पादने द्विगुणःshould have according to the Malayalam commentary some more supplementary sūtras. V.A. Ramasvami Sastri has reconstructed the portion based on Malayalam commentary as follows⁴¹: ब्रणविदारणे चतुर्गुणः, शाखाङ्गच्छेदने अष्टचत्वारिंशत्पणः। लिङ्गस्य छेदने मृत्यौ च मध्यमः साहसखण्डः समुत्थानव्ययञ्च ।मरणे गोमूल्यं पूर्वश्च साहसदण्डः।

 The editor notes that there is a stanza in the *Yājñavalkyasmṛti* prescribing the above punishment. This only shows that the commentary is too valuable to be ignored by any student of *Arthaśāstra.*

*Similarity*: The similarity between the *Pratipadapañcikā* of Bhaṭṭasvāmi and the Malayalam commentary is inescapable. Even though the commentator might have seen the other

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

⁴⁰. *TSS* 80, p. 109

⁴¹. *Kauṭalīya* with Malayalam commentary ed. V.A. Ramasvami Sastri, Pt. III, p. 123



commentaries, he has been more influenced by *Pratipada-pañcikā*. An instance may be noted to bring out the striking similarity between the two.

Bhaṭṭasvāmi on Adhikaraṇa II Adhyāya 14 :

 विशिखायां सौर्वाणिकप्रचार इति सूत्रम् ।विशिखारथ्या च सामान्याभिधानेऽपि सौवर्णिकः विपणिमार्गः परिगृह्यते । तस्यां विशिखायाम् । सुवर्णे नियुक्तःसौवर्णिकः।तस्य प्रचारो व्यापारोविशिखायां सौवार्णिकप्रचारः। स इहाभिषीयत इति सूत्रार्थः। संबन्धस्तु कोशप्रकृतिप्रक्रान्तस्य राजकीयसुवर्णरूप्यघटनाद्युपदेशप्रकारे रक्षणमुक्तम्।

Malayalam commentary on Adhikaraṇa II Adhyāya 14: “Viśikhāyām sauvarṇikapracāra enpitu sūtram. Viśikhāyāvitu nālkkavala. Atināl niyuktanāya sauvarṇikanuṭaya vyāpāram collinṭatu enpitu sūtrārtham. Sambandhamāvitu kīle adhyāyattil abhijātnumāy śilpaṅkaḷum valli terappeṭuvitum ceytu janapadādiguṇankaleyum uṭayanāyirikkum sauvarṇikanai nālkkavalayil vaikkumāru collītu, avanuṭaya vyāpāram collītilla. Anantaram vyāpāram collinṭatu enpitu sambandham”.

 Thus numerous instances of similarities can be noticed between the two commentaries. But very often the Malayalam commentator has omitted many portions of Bhaṭṭasvāmi. What the latter has explained in details is seen dismissed often altogether by the former as though it is evident and needs no explanation. On the other hand he has ventured on certain instances where Bhaṭṭasvāmi is silent. In other words the Malayalam commentator has kept an open mind and exhibits his sense of judgement in accepting the Sanskrit commentator. The *Jayamaṅgalā* commentary is more detailed than Pratipadapañcikā. The Malayalam commentator has, occasionally drawn upon this commentary when he wants to give more details than Bhaṭṭasvāmi’s comments. An instance of similarity may be cited as follows :

*Jayamaṅgalā*on Adhikaraṇa I Adhyāya 7:

 मर्यादां स्थापयेत् आचार्यानमात्यान् वेति । मर्यादामिव सीमामिव अनतिक्रमणीयत्वात् । य एनमिति । ये आचार्या अमात्या वा एनं राजानं अपायस्थानेभ्यःयत्रापाया अवश्यं सन्निहिताः तेभ्यो वारयेयुःयुष्माभिरहं प्रसङ्गेभ्यो

निवारणीय इति । तत्राचार्याःधर्मविरोधात्, अमात्याःअर्थविरोधात्वारयन्ति।

 The Malayalam commentary is on the same lines borowing expressions from the predecessor.

“Maryādamāyi acāryanmāreyum amātyareyum sthāpippatu. Maryādam enṭavāru atir. Atirkkaṇakke alaṅghanīyamākinṭamaiyāl ivaraḷayum maryādamenṭu collintaṭu. Yāvar cilar rājāvinē apāyasthānattil ninṭu vilakkuvor enṭavāru anarthani-mittamāyuḷḷa anuṣṭhānamolippor”.

(Adhikaraṇa I Adhyāya 7)

 Among the other commentaries which show some affinity with the Malayalam commentary may be included the *Cāṇakyaṭīkā* of Bhiksuprabhamati. But his dependence is rather limited when compared to the other two.

 There is no doubt that the Malayalam commentator was anative of Kerala. He was familiar with the topography and customs of the region. In dealing with the different places from where pearls are obtained he explains “Caurṇeyam” of the text stating that it is procured from the river Cūrṇī flowing through the town Muracī in Kerala. The place is identified with the modern Koṭuṅṅallūr in north Kerala. The following passage is to the point⁴².“Caurneyamavitu malanāṭṭil muraci ākinṭa paṭṭinattinnarikē cūrṇiyāṭṭil uḷavāmavu” River Cūrṇī also known at present as Periyār alias. Alwaye river is one of the biggest among the
rivers of Kerala with a length of 142 miles. The matrilineal system prevalent among the Keralites is mentioned by him in explaining the passage: स्वधर्माछायाद्याद्वोपरुद्धः।

The portion comes in for a detailed comment as follows :⁴³“Svadharmamāvitu dākṣiṇātyarkku mātulakanyāvivāhādikaḷ; dāyādamāvitu dākṣiṇāṭyarkkē tammāman dhanam marumakkaḷ  
koḷḷumatu; aṅṅaneyoḷḷa dharmattilum cerukkappeṭṭavan”.

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

⁴². *kauṭaliyam* Malayalam commentary, pt. II, p. 93

⁴³ . *Ibid* pt.I. p.115

Bhikṣuprabhamati has explained it simply as paternal heritage.

*Date* : Regarding the date of this ancient Malayalam commentary there is some uncertainty. The identity of the author itself is vague. K. Sambasiva Sastri is of the opinion that the author who is well-versed in political craft must either be a local ruler or a minister with practical knowledge of state-craft. The above-mentioned reference to matriarchy and the customs prevalent in the South is suggestive of his having been a native of the South, preferably of Kerala. He does not venture to suggest any date to the author. Instead he prefers to accept the date suggested by Ullur S. Parameswara Iyer, the great historian of Kerala literature. Ullur holds the view that it must have been composed during the period between 9th and 10th centuries when the Cera kings were holding the reins. The anonymous author must have composed the work at the suggestion of his king⁴⁴ K.N. Ezhuthachan relies more on the nature of the language in fixing the date of the work. He too believes that the work might have been written by somebody proficient in statecraft. He draws our attention to the court of the kings of Quilon like that of the famous Ravivarmā Saṁgrāmadhīra who flourished in the 13th century A.D. The anonymous commentator was patronished by some predecessor of the said king. According to him 12th century must be the probable date of composition. In holding this view he relies on the view of Prof. Elankulam Kunjan Pillai⁴⁵ who suggests a period between 1150 to 1200 A D. based on linguistic features. This view has found general acceptance at the hands of later scholars.

**(7) *ŚRIMULAM***

 Mahāmahopādhyāya Dr. T. Ganapati Sastri has given for the first time a complete commentary of Arthaśāstra in Sanskrit utilising his rich experience as the famous editor of the Trivandrum Sanskrit Series. He has given to his commentary

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

^(44.)Ullur. *op. cit,* p.269

^(45.)P.N.Kunjan Pillai, *Keralabhāṣyuṭe Vikāsapariṇāmaṅṅal,*part1 (Revised edn), Kottayam,1959, p.92

the significant name *Śrīmūlam*—the source of prosperity—after the name of his patron Śrīmulam Tirunāl Mahārājā of Travancore to whom the work is dedicated.

 In his introduction to the three-part edition of Arthaśāstra he has justified his attempt in bringing out a new edition and writing a new commentary. The limitation of Shama Sastri’s edition based on a single manuscript is too well known. The ong experience as Curator helped Ganapati Sastri to unearth five manuscripts of the work without much effort though he used only four of them since the fifth was decayed and beyond use. Copies of three incomplete commentaries on the text, *Pratipadapañcikā* of Bhaṭṭasvāmi, *Nayacandrikā* of Mādhavayajvan and an anonymous Malayalam commentary were also procured. With this nearly sufficient material he embarked on the stupendous project of editing and preparing a commentary on Kauṭalya's *Arthaśāstra.* It was a Herculean task considering the Sastraic nature of the work and it was easily. the finest editorial attempt in the career of Ganapati Sastri.

 By the time the author had gained rich experience and the publication was one of the last efforts of the great scholar. The last and third part of the work was published in 1925 and in the next year in April 1926 he breathed his last.

 *Life*: A peep into the life and career of this savant is rewarding to any one interested in literary pursuits. Taruvai Ganapati Sastri was born in 1860 in the village of Taruvai in the TiruneIveli District. He was the son of Rāmasubrahmanialyer—a descendant of the illustrious family of the celebrated Appayya Dīkṣita. His mother was a pious lady by name Sītāmbā. In his *Aparṇāstava* he has referred to his native place on the banks of the river Tāmraparṇi as follows:

**गणपतिरिति कश्चिद् ब्राह्मणस्ताम्रपर्णी-  
तटजुषि तरुवानाम्न्यप्रहारेऽभिजातः॥**

A similar stanza occurs at the end of his *Śrīmūlam* commentary as follows:

**गरणपतिरिति कश्चिद् ब्राह्मणस्ताम्रपर्णी-**

**तटजुषि तरुवैनाम्न्यग्रहारेऽभिजातः।**

**नरपतिनयसर्वस्वोपदेशप्रधानं**

**व्यवृत विपुलयत्नःकौटलीयार्थशास्त्रम् ॥**

In a colophon to his commentary on *Svapnavāsavadatta* he has mentioned his parentage as well as his native village in the following lines : ताम्रपर्णीतीरवर्तितरुवाग्रहाराभिजनस्य श्रीसीताम्बारामसुब्रह्मण्यार्यसूनोर्गणपतिशास्त्रिरणः कृतिषु स्वप्नवासवदत्तव्याख्यानं सम्पूर्णम् ।

Towards the end of the 15th Adhikaraṇa of the commentary occur the following remarks:

 “इति काञ्चीमण्डलमहाराजाभिमतस्य ताम्रपर्णीतीरवर्तितरुवाग्रहाराभिजनस्य श्रीसीताम्बारामसुब्रह्मण्यार्यसूनो- र्महोपाध्यायगणपतिशास्त्रिणः कृतिषु कौटलीयार्थशास्त्रव्याख्यायां श्रीमूलव्याख्यायाम् —।”

 Born of poor parents he had the benefit of some early education in Sanskrit under a teacher named Nīlakaṇṭha Śāstrī. By the age of sixteen he acquired some Proficiency in Sanskrit, but he could not find suitable avocation to sustain his family. The young man set out from his village on foot in search of patronage and soon reached Trivandrum, the capital of Travancore kings celebrated for their benevolence. Ganapati Sastri found a house for him in Chalai village. There he became acquainted with Kaḍayam Subbayya Dīkṣitar, an authority in Sanskrit grammar who was only pleased to teach the young aspirant. With his help the youngster mastered grammar and poetics. Another well known teacher of the period  
Dharmādhikāri Karmani Subramanian Śāstri helped him to attain proficienty in many Sastraic treatises. The student impressed his teachers with his extraordinary brilliance and keen observation. He worked his way up by sheer force of his scholarship, industry and character to an international fame.

 His official career began in 1871 at the age of 19 when he joined the High Court of Travancore as a Junior Assistant, in which position he served for about ten years. In 1889 the Trivandrum Sanskrit College was established and Ganapati Sastri was appointed as one of the early teachers. Soon he became the Headmaster of the institution and gradually rose to the coveted position of the principal of the only Sanskrit College in the State. In was in the year 1899. But his contact with Sanskrit manuscripts began much earlier. In 1897 Viśākham Tirunāl Mahārājā appointed him the keeper of the

prestigious Palace Library and in this capacity he came into contact with great literary figures and scholars of the period including Keralavarma Valiyakoyil Tampurān — the Kālidāsa of Kerala, and Illattūr Rāmasvāmi Śāstri, a court poet of the illustrious Travancore rulers. Since he was very much interested in manuscripts, he continued to hold the advantageous position even after rising to the positon of the principal. Impressed by the enthusiasm of Ganapati Sastri, in the year 1908 the Government decided to establish a separate department for the Publication of ancient manuscripts. He was rightly chosen to head this new department to which be brought international reputation within a few years. He occupied the position of the Curator for a period of sixteen years and was due to retire at the age of 55 in 1915. It was at about that time that he launched the project of editing *Arthaśāstra* to add a new feather to his cap. Naturally his tenure of service was extended on an annual basis for a record period of ten years. At last in the year 1925 after the third and last part of the *Arthaśāstra* was published, he voluntarily retired from service due to ill-health. In the following year at the age of 67 he passed away on the 3rd April 1926. *Scholarship* : He began his literary career at the age of 17 and his first work was a Sanskrit drama called *Madhvīvāsantīya*, for which his patron awarded him a gold ring. Though his editorial preoccupations and official duties did not permit him to contribute much, he has to his credit about 14 original compositions as follows: (1) *Mādhvīvāsantīyam—*a drama, (2) *Aparaṇāstava—*a hymn in praise of Durgā to which he also wrote a commentary, (3) *Śrīmūlacaritam—*a kavya on the history of Travancore during the reign of his patron Śrīmūla Tirunāl Rāmavarma Mahārājā, (4) *Bhārat*ā*nuvarṇanam—*a historical account of India to which Prof. Sylvain Levi contributed a foreword, (5) *Tulāpuruṣadānam—*a poem dealing with the Tulābhāra ceremony of his patron king, (6) *Cacravartinīguṇamaṇimālā—*a poem in praise of Queen Victoria, (8) *Arthacitramaṇimālā—*a treatise on poetics with illustrative stanzas on Viśākham Tirunāl Mahārājā of Travancore, (8) *Setuyātrāvarṇanam—*a prose work dealing with his pilgrimage to Rāmośvaram. (9) *Arthaśāstravyākhyā* styled *Śrīmūlam* being a

complete commentary on Kauṭalya's monumental work. (10) *Śākuntalapāramayavyākhvā—*a commentary on a treatise by Keralavarma Valya Koyil Tampurān. (11) *Viśākhavijayatīppaṇi—* a gloss on the historical mahākāvya glorifying Viśākham. Tirunāl written by Keralavarma, (12) *Āṅglasāmrājyatippaṇi—*a commentary on the historical poem by A.R. Rajarājavarma known as the Pāṇini of Kerala, (13) *Gandharvasallāpa—*a poem in lighter vein, and (14) *Svapnavāsavadattavyākhyānam—*a comprehensive commentary on the Bhāsa play discovered and published by him for the first time. As a commentator his attainment is of a high order and his talent in this respect came in for international acclaim. The one he wrote on *Arthaśāstra* is considered to be a classic and it is a significant contribution to that text on Indian polity.

 As the founder Curator of the Trivandrum Manuscripts Library and Editor of the renowned Trivandrum Sanskrit Series, he put Trivandrum on the map of Oriental research. A scholar observed that “among Pandits who took to research a few attained the eminence of Ganapati Sastri and among research scholars themselves few ever got that measure of recognition and honour that Sastri received during the course of his career”. He brought out 87 publications in the Trivandrum Sanskrit Series of which 68 were his on editorial efforts with useful introductions. More than this; the discovery of the so-called Bhāsa plays made him world-famous. It took the scholarly world by storm as it were. When opposition arose questioning his findings, he stoutly faced the criticism and answered them elaborately. The Buddhist Tantric work *Āryamauñjuśrīmūlakalpa* from which the late Dr. K.P. Jayaswal extracted so much valuable historical information was yet another epoch-making editorial effort of Ganapati Sastri. The *Samarāṅgaṇasūtradhāra* of Bhoja edited by him for the Gaekewad’s Oriental Series is a store-house of wonderful information about ancient Indian arts, crafts and machines.

 Recognition sought him from the different parts of India and abroad. He was bestowed with the prestigious title *Mahāmahopādhvāva* by the British Government. Royal Asiatic

Society made him an honorary member. In 1924. the year in which he brought out the first part of the *Arthaśāstra* the Tubingea University of Germany presented him with an Honorary Doctorate as a tribute to his unique scholarship. In the third part of the work which appeared in 1925, Ganapati Sastri has added the rare title and degree to his name.

*Śrīmūlam* : The *Śrīmūlam* commentary mostly owes its material to three early commentaries. As admitted by Ganapati Sastri fragments of ancient commentaries— that of *Pratipadapañcikā* of Bhaṭṭasvāmī, *Nayacandrik*ā of Mādhavayajvan and an old Malayalam commentary— were obtained by him. Since no ancient commentary was available fit enough to be published, he thought of writing his own gloss. He was conscious of the importance of traditional commentaries in understanding a text of this nature. He admits that owing to its technical nature, the meaning can be grasped only with the help of traditional instructions or commentaries and that “in the absence of these materials our ignorance in regard to many topics would be perpetual”.

* Arthaśāstra* as already noticed consists of 15 Adhikaraṇas and 150 Adhyāyas. The three commentaries mentioned above and procured by him supplied him with the material for almost 12 Adhikaraṇas and 139 Adhyāyas. In other words he was left on his own for 3 Adhikaraṇas and 11 Adhyāyas. While the ancient Malayalam commentary provided him with the material for the first seven Adhikaraṇas consisting of 116 Adhyāyas, *Nayacandrikā* of Mah*ā*yajvan supplied him the basis for almost 6 Adhikaraṇas from the 7th Adhikaraṇa upto the 4th Adhyāya of the 12th Adhikaraṇa. Thus the second commentary was available to him for 37 Adhy*ā*yas from 103 to 139 out of a total of 150. Bhaṭṭasv*ā*mī’s commentary for the last 29 chapters of the second Adhikaraṇa helped him to have a comparative assessment of the Malayalam commentary and the Sanskrit commentary. This was the material on which Ganapati Sastri built up the monumental structure of *Śrīmūlam* commentary. The experience that he gained in editing almost 90 per cent of the work stood him in good stead to complete the task by composing commentary on the last 11 chapters of

the book. He considered that it was his good fortune to get these early commentaries. Till today we have not come across any ancient commentary dealing with the last three Adhikaraṇas and as such Ganapati Sastri’s gloss remains the only material to help the students of *Arthaśāstra.*

 Obviously Ganapati Sastri did not have a high opinion about the Mysore edition of the work published by Shama Sastri in 1909. He found that “It abounded with errors, such as, the word of a previous sentence joined to a subsequent sentence and vice-versa.” Further, he observes that the English translation of Shama Sastri misrepresents the facts here and there. That too prompted him to think on the lines of composing a new commentary since the purification of the text alone will not suffice. Towards the end of introduction, he has hinted that “a scholarly friend, who has given many years to the study of *Arthaśāstra*” will provide a new English translation on the basis of his edition. But this hope remains unfulfilled.

*Influence* of *Bhaṭṭasvāmī* : It has already been noted that Bhaṭṭasvāmī’s commentary has been thoroughly made use of by the *Śrīmūlam* commentator. A close study of the two reveals that Ganapati Sastri has quoted 37 stanzas from the commentary of Bhaṭṭasvāmī including the two stanzas quoted by the latter from *Bārhaspatya*. The 37 stanzas are quoted in the first Part of the *Śrīmūlam* commentary on pages noted below :

Of these the two stanzas on p. 229 are quotations from *Bārhaspatya* made by Bhaṭṭasvāmī. The two stanzas on p. 248 are also found quoted in the Malayalam commentary⁴⁶. In addition to these stanzas Bhaṭṭasvāmī’s explanations are also quoted at time in the *Śrīmūlam commentary*, as for instance on I. 239 he quotes : **तच्चत्रिविधं, मनुष्यप्रवर्तितं बलीबर्दप्रवर्तितं सलिलप्रवर्तितं चेति भट्टस्वामी ।**

*Indebtedness to Malayalam commentary*:The indebtedness of the *Śrīmūlam* commentary to the ancient Malayalam commentary has been admitted by Ganapati Sastri in his introduction. But in the course of his commentary it is not adequately mentioned as it really deserves. We occasionally come across some reference to the *Bhāṣāṭīkā* as in I. 225. Otherwise the editor is silent even when he quotes stanzas from the Malayalam commentary. In the 7 Adhikaraṇas of the Malayalam commentary 22 stanzas are found quoted or composed by the author. Of these only two are known to be quotations from Bhaṭṭasvāmī’s commentary as in 248. All the other 20 stanzas are either composed by the Malayalam commentary, 22 stanzas are found quoted or composed by the author. Of these only two are known to be quotations from Bhaṭṭasvāmī’s commentary as in I. 248. All the other 20 stanzas are either composed by the Malayalam commentator or quoted by him from anonymous sources. Ganapati Sastri has quoted all these 22 stanzas without mentioning the *Bhāṣātīkā*. These occur in the *Śrīmūlam* commentary as follows :
